<tr>
<td colspan="2" class="noprint selfref" style="text-align:center;background-color:#ddddff;padding:0.3em 0.5em;text-align:left;line-height:1.3;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a> phonetic symbols.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet#Technical_note" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Mojibake" title="Mojibake">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> characters.</td>

<p><b>English</b> is a <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic language</a> that was first spoken in <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_England" title="Anglo-Saxon England" class="mw-redirect">early medieval England</a> and is now a global <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a6_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a6-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWardhaugh201055_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWardhaugh201055-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> It is an <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> of <a href="/wiki/List_of_territorial_entities_where_English_is_an_official_language" title="List of territorial entities where English is an official language">almost 60 sovereign states</a>, the most commonly spoken language in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, and a widely spoken language in countries in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>, Africa, and South Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003b108.E2.80.93109_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003b108.E2.80.93109-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> It is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_number_of_native_speakers" title="List of languages by number of native speakers">third most common native language</a> in the world, after <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Mandarin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEthnologue2010_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEthnologue2010-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> It is widely learned as a <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">second language</a> and is an <a href="/wiki/Official_languages_of_the_United_Nations" title="Official languages of the United Nations">official language of the United Nations</a>, of <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_European_Union" title="Languages of the European Union">the European Union</a>, and of many other world and regional international organisations.</p>
<p>English has developed over the course of more than 1,400 years. The earliest forms of English, a set of <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Frisian_languages" title="Anglo-Frisian languages">Anglo-Frisian dialects</a> brought to <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> by <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxon settlers</a> in the fifth century, are called <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>. <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> began in the late 11th century with the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest of England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003b30_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003b30-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_English" title="Early Modern English">Early Modern English</a> began in the late 15th century with the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> to London and the <a href="/wiki/King_James_Bible" title="King James Bible" class="mw-redirect">King James Bible</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Vowel_Shift" title="Great Vowel Shift">Great Vowel Shift</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Through the worldwide influence of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, modern English spread around the world from the 17th to mid-20th centuries. Through newspapers, books, the telegraph, the telephone, phonograph records, radio, satellite television, and the Internet, as well as the emergence of the United States as a global <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">superpower</a>, English has become the <a href="/wiki/World_language" title="World language">leading language</a> of international discourse and the lingua franca in many regions and in professional contexts such as science.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Routes_of_English2015_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Routes_of_English2015-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>There is little <a href="/wiki/Inflectional_morphology" title="Inflectional morphology" class="mw-redirect">morphological inflection</a> in Modern English, and the syntax is generally <a href="/wiki/Isolating_language" title="Isolating language">isolating</a>. English relies on auxiliary verbs and word order for the expression of complex tenses, aspect and mood, as well as passive constructions, interrogatives and negation. Despite noticeable variation between the forms of English spoken in different world regions, English-speakers from around the world can communicate with one another effectively. Different accents are distinguished only by phonological and phonetic differences among speakers, whereas dialects also display grammatical and lexical differences.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Westgermanic_English_tree.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Westgermanic_English_tree.svg/400px-Westgermanic_English_tree.svg.png" width="400" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Westgermanic_English_tree.svg/600px-Westgermanic_English_tree.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Westgermanic_English_tree.svg/800px-Westgermanic_English_tree.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="872" data-file-height="457" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Westgermanic_English_tree.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">Phylogenetic tree</a> showing the historical relations between the languages of the West Germanic branch of the Germanic languages.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>English is an <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European language</a>, and belongs to the <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">West Germanic</a> group of the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBammesberger199229.E2.80.9330_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBammesberger199229.E2.80.9330-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Most closely related to English are the <a href="/wiki/Frisian_languages" title="Frisian languages">Frisian languages</a>, and English and Frisian form the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Frisian_languages" title="Anglo-Frisian languages">Anglo-Frisian</a> subgroup within West Germanic. <a href="/wiki/Old_Saxon" title="Old Saxon">Old Saxon</a> and its descendent <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">Low German</a> languages are also closely related, and sometimes Low German, English, and Frisian are grouped together as the <a href="/wiki/Ingvaeonic_languages" title="Ingvaeonic languages">Ingvaeonic</a> or North Sea Germanic languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBammesberger199230_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBammesberger199230-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Modern English descends from <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a>, which in turn descends from <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson1992_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson1992-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> English and all the Germanic languages descend from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic</a>. Middle English also developed into a number of other <a href="/wiki/English_languages" title="English languages">English languages</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine198256.E2.80.9365_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine198256.E2.80.9365-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> and the extinct <a href="/wiki/Fingallian" title="Fingallian">Fingallian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forth_and_Bargy_dialect" title="Forth and Bargy dialect">Forth and Bargy dialects</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarry198286.E2.80.9387_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarry198286.E2.80.9387-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>English is classified as a Germanic language because it shares new language features (different from other Indo-European languages) with other Germanic languages such as <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDurrell2006_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDurrell2006-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> These shared innovations show that the languages have descended from a single common ancestor, which linguists call Proto-Germanic. Some shared features of Germanic languages are the use of <a href="/wiki/Modal_verb" title="Modal verb">modal verbs</a>, the division of verbs into <a href="/wiki/Germanic_strong_verb" title="Germanic strong verb">strong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germanic_weak_verb" title="Germanic weak verb">weak</a> classes, and the sound changes affecting <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> consonants, known as <a href="/wiki/Grimm%27s_law" title="Grimm's law">Grimm's</a> and <a href="/wiki/Verner%27s_law" title="Verner's law">Verner's laws</a>. Through Grimm's law, the word for <i>foot</i> begins with <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/f/</span> in Germanic languages, but its <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognates</a> in other Indo-European languages begin with <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/p/</span>. English is classified as an Anglo-Frisian language because Frisian and English share other features, such as the <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">palatalisation</a> of consonants that were velar consonants in Proto-Germanic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6nigvan_der_Auwera1994_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6nigvan_der_Auwera1994-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>English <i>sing</i>, <i>sang</i>, <i>sung</i>; Dutch <i>zingen</i>, <i>zong</i>, <i>gezongen</i>; German <i>singen</i>, <i>sang</i>, <i>gesungen</i> (strong verb)</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>English <i>laugh</i>, <i>laughed</i>; Dutch and German <i>lachen</i>, <i>lachte</i> (weak verb)</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>English <i>foot</i>, German <i>Fuß</i>, Norwegian and Swedish <i>fot</i> (initial <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/f/</span> derived from Proto-Indo-European <span title="Proto-Indo-European language" class="Unicode" lang="ine" style="white-space: nowrap">*p</span> through Grimm's law)</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>Latin <i>pes</i>, <a href="/wiki/Word_stem" title="Word stem">stem</a> <i>ped-</i>; Modern Greek <span lang="el">πόδι</span> <i>pódi</i>; <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> <span lang="ru">под</span> <i>pod</i>; <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <span lang="sa">पद्</span> <i>pád</i> (original Proto-Indo-European <span title="Proto-Indo-European language" class="Unicode" lang="ine" style="white-space: nowrap">*p</span>)</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>English <i>cheese</i>, Frisian <i>tsiis</i> (<i>ch</i> and <i>ts</i> from palatalisation)</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>German <i>Käse</i> and Dutch <i>kaas</i> (<i>k</i> without palatalisation)</dd>
</dl>
<p>English, like the other insular Germanic languages, <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">Faroese</a>, developed independently of the continental Germanic languages and their influences. English is thus not <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutually intelligible</a> with any continental Germanic language, differing in <a href="/wiki/Lexis_(linguistics)" title="Lexis (linguistics)">vocabulary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a>, although some, such as Dutch, do show strong affinities with English, especially with its earlier stages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarbert2007_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarbert2007-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Because English through its history has changed considerably in response to contact with other languages, particularly <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norman_language" title="Norman language">Norman French</a>, some scholars have argued that English can be considered a <a href="/wiki/Mixed_language" title="Mixed language">mixed language</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Creole_language" title="Creole language">creole</a>&#160;– a theory called the <a href="/wiki/Middle_English_creole_hypothesis" title="Middle English creole hypothesis">Middle English creole hypothesis</a>. Although the high degree of influence from these languages on the vocabulary and grammar of Modern English is widely acknowledged, most specialists in language contact do not consider English to be a true mixed language.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomasonKaufman1988264.E2.80.93265_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomasonKaufman1988264.E2.80.93265-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatts2011Chapter_4_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatts2011Chapter_4-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_English_language" title="History of the English language">History of the English language</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proto-Germanic_to_Old_English">Proto-Germanic to Old English</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beowulf_Cotton_MS_Vitellius_A_XV_f._132r.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Beowulf_Cotton_MS_Vitellius_A_XV_f._132r.jpg/220px-Beowulf_Cotton_MS_Vitellius_A_XV_f._132r.jpg" width="220" height="369" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Beowulf_Cotton_MS_Vitellius_A_XV_f._132r.jpg/330px-Beowulf_Cotton_MS_Vitellius_A_XV_f._132r.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Beowulf_Cotton_MS_Vitellius_A_XV_f._132r.jpg/440px-Beowulf_Cotton_MS_Vitellius_A_XV_f._132r.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4098" data-file-height="6868" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beowulf_Cotton_MS_Vitellius_A_XV_f._132r.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The opening to the Old English epic poem <i><a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Manuscript" title="Manuscript">handwritten</a> in <a href="/wiki/Uncial_script#Half-uncial" title="Uncial script">half-uncial script</a>:<br />
<i><span lang="ang">Hƿæt ƿē Gārde/na ingēar dagum þēod cyninga / þrym ge frunon...</span></i><br />
"Listen! We of the Spear-Danes from days of yore have heard of the glory of the folk-kings..."</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a></div>
<p>The earliest form of English is called Old English or Anglo-Saxon (c.&#160;550–1066&#160;CE). Old English developed from a set of <a href="/wiki/Ingvaeonic_languages" title="Ingvaeonic languages">North Sea Germanic dialects</a> originally spoken along the coasts of <a href="/wiki/Frisia" title="Frisia">Frisia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lower_Saxony" title="Lower Saxony">Lower Saxony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jutland" title="Jutland">Jutland</a>, and Southern <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> by Germanic tribes known as the <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jutes" title="Jutes">Jutes</a>. In the fifth century, the Anglo-Saxons <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_settlement_of_Britain" title="Anglo-Saxon settlement of Britain">settled Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/End_of_Roman_rule_in_Britain" title="End of Roman rule in Britain">the Romans withdrew from Britain</a>. By the seventh century, the Germanic language of the Anglo-Saxons became dominant in Britain, replacing the languages of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a> (43–409 CE): <a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">Common Brittonic</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic language</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, brought to Britain by the <a href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_invasions_of_Britain" title="Caesar's invasions of Britain">Roman occupation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollingwoodMyres1936_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollingwoodMyres1936-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraddolLeithSwann_et_al.2007_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraddolLeithSwann_et_al.2007-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlenchSpriggs1999_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlenchSpriggs1999-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>England</i> and <i>English</i> (originally <i>Englaland</i> and <i>Englisc</i>) are named after the Angles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBosworthToller1921_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBosworthToller1921-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Old English was divided into four dialects: the Anglian dialects, <a href="/wiki/Mercian_dialect" title="Mercian dialect">Mercian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northumbrian_dialect" title="Northumbrian dialect">Northumbrian</a>, and the Saxon dialects, <a href="/wiki/Kentish_dialect" title="Kentish dialect">Kentish</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Saxon_dialect" title="West Saxon dialect">West Saxon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell19594_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell19594-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> Through the educational reforms of <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">King Alfred</a> in the ninth century and the influence of the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a>, the West Saxon dialect became the <a href="/wiki/Standard_language" title="Standard language">standard written variety</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEToon1992Chapter:_Old_English_Dialects_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEToon1992Chapter:_Old_English_Dialects-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">epic poem</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a></i> is written in West Saxon, and the earliest English poem, <i><a href="/wiki/C%C3%A6dmon%27s_Hymn" title="Cædmon's Hymn">Cædmon's Hymn</a></i>, is written in Northumbrian.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDonoghue2008_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDonoghue2008-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> Modern English developed mainly from Mercian, but the <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots language</a> developed from Northumbrian. A few short inscriptions from the early period of Old English were written using a <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_runes" title="Anglo-Saxon runes">runic script</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGneuss201323_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGneuss201323-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> By the sixth century, a <a href="/wiki/Old_English_Latin_alphabet" title="Old English Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> was adopted, written with <a href="/wiki/Uncial_script#Half-uncial" title="Uncial script">half-uncial</a> <a href="/wiki/Letterform" title="Letterform">letterforms</a>. It included the runic letters <i><a href="/wiki/Wynn" title="Wynn">wynn</a></i> <span lang="ang">&lt;ƿ&gt;</span> and <i><a href="/wiki/Thorn_(letter)" title="Thorn (letter)">thorn</a></i> <span lang="ang">&lt;þ&gt;</span>, and the modified Latin letters <i><a href="/wiki/Eth" title="Eth">eth</a></i> <span lang="ang">&lt;ð&gt;</span>, and <i><a href="/wiki/%C3%86" title="Æ">ash</a></i> <span lang="ang">&lt;æ&gt;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGneuss201323_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGneuss201323-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDenisonHogg200630.E2.80.9331_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDenisonHogg200630.E2.80.9331-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Old English is very different from Modern English and difficult for 21st-century English speakers to understand. Its grammar was similar to that of modern <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>. <a href="/wiki/Old_English_grammar" title="Old English grammar">Nouns, adjectives, pronouns, and verbs</a> had many more <a href="/wiki/Inflection#Inflectional_morphology" title="Inflection">inflectional endings and forms</a>, and word order was <a href="/wiki/Word_order#Pragmatic_word_order" title="Word order">much freer</a> than in Modern English. Modern English has <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">case forms</a> in pronouns (<i>he</i>, <i>him</i>, <i>his</i>) and a few verb endings (<i>I have</i>, <i>he has</i>), but Old English had case endings in nouns as well, and verbs had more <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">person</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">number</a> endings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHogg1992Chapter_3._Phonology_and_Morphology_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHogg1992Chapter_3._Phonology_and_Morphology-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith2009_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith2009-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETraskTrask2010_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETraskTrask2010-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The translation of Matthew 8:20 from 1000&#160;CE shows examples of case endings (<a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">nominative</a> plural, <a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative</a> plural, <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a> singular) and a verb ending (<a href="/wiki/Present_tense" title="Present tense">present</a> plural):</p>
<dl>
<dd><i>Foxas habbað holu and heofonan fuglas nest</i></dd>
<dd>Fox-as habb-að hol-u and heofon-an fugl-as nest-∅</dd>
<dd>fox-<span class="smallcaps"><span style="FONT-VARIANT: SMALL-CAPS; TEXT-TRANSFORM: LOWERCASE;">NOM.PL</span></span> have-<span class="smallcaps"><span style="FONT-VARIANT: SMALL-CAPS; TEXT-TRANSFORM: LOWERCASE;">PRS.PL</span></span> hole-<span class="smallcaps"><span style="FONT-VARIANT: SMALL-CAPS; TEXT-TRANSFORM: LOWERCASE;">ACC.PL</span></span> and heaven-<span class="smallcaps"><span style="FONT-VARIANT: SMALL-CAPS; TEXT-TRANSFORM: LOWERCASE;">GEN.SG</span></span> bird-<span class="smallcaps"><span style="FONT-VARIANT: SMALL-CAPS; TEXT-TRANSFORM: LOWERCASE;">NOM.PL</span></span> nest-<span class="smallcaps"><span style="FONT-VARIANT: SMALL-CAPS; TEXT-TRANSFORM: LOWERCASE;">ACC.PL</span></span></dd>
<dd>"Foxes have holes and the birds of heaven nests"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELass200646.E2.80.9347_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELass200646.E2.80.9347-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_English">Middle English</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a></div>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:300px; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div style="margin:0px 2em;">
<p><i>Englischmen þeyz hy hadde fram þe bygynnyng þre manner speche, Souþeron, Northeron, and Myddel speche in þe myddel of þe lond, ... Noþeles by comyxstion and mellyng, furst wiþ Danes, and afterward wiþ Normans, in menye þe contray longage ys asperyed, and som vseþ strange wlaffyng, chyteryng, harryng, and garryng grisbytting</i>.<br />
<br />
Although, from the beginning, Englishmen had three manners of speaking, southern, northern and midlands speech in the middle of the country, ... Nevertheless, through intermingling and mixing, first with Danes and then with Normans, amongst many the country language has arisen, and some use strange stammering, chattering, snarling, and grating gnashing.</p>
</div>
<div style="position: absolute; left: 0px; top: 0px; text-align: left; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; font-size: large; color: gray;">“</div>
<div style="position: absolute; right: 0px; bottom: 0px; text-align: left; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; font-size: large; color: gray;">”</div>
</div>
<div style="text-align: right;"><cite><a href="/wiki/John_of_Trevisa" title="John of Trevisa" class="mw-redirect">John of Trevisa</a>, ca. 1385<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHogg2006360.E2.80.93361_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHogg2006360.E2.80.93361-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></cite></div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hengwrt_Chaucer_(f.2.r)_title_page.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Hengwrt_Chaucer_%28f.2.r%29_title_page.jpg/180px-Hengwrt_Chaucer_%28f.2.r%29_title_page.jpg" width="180" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Hengwrt_Chaucer_%28f.2.r%29_title_page.jpg/270px-Hengwrt_Chaucer_%28f.2.r%29_title_page.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Hengwrt_Chaucer_%28f.2.r%29_title_page.jpg/360px-Hengwrt_Chaucer_%28f.2.r%29_title_page.jpg 2x" data-file-width="646" data-file-height="850" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hengwrt_Chaucer_(f.2.r)_title_page.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Title page of Geoffrey Chaucer's <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Tales" title="Canterbury Tales" class="mw-redirect">Canterbury Tales</a> c.1400</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the period from the 8th to the 12th century, Old English gradually transformed through <a href="/wiki/Language_contact" title="Language contact">language contact</a> into <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a>. Middle English is often arbitrarily defined as beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">conquest of England</a> by <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a> in 1066, but it developed further in the period from 1200–1450.</p>
<p>First, the waves of Norse colonisation of northern parts of the British Isles in the 8th and 9th centuries put Old English into intense contact with <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a>, a <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic" title="North Germanic" class="mw-redirect">North Germanic</a> language. Norse influence was strongest in the Northeastern varieties of Old English spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">Danelaw</a> area around York, which was the centre of Norse colonisation; today these features are still particularly present in <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_England_English" title="Northern England English" class="mw-redirect">Northern English</a>. However the centre of norsified English seems to have been in <a href="/wiki/The_Midlands" title="The Midlands">the Midlands</a> around <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Lindsey" title="Kingdom of Lindsey">Lindsey</a>, and after 920 CE when Lindsey was reincorporated into the Anglo-Saxon polity, Norse features spread from there into English varieties that had not been in intense contact with Norse speakers. Some elements of Norse influence that persist in all English varieties today are the pronouns beginning with <i>th-</i> (<i>they, them, their</i>) which replaced the Anglo-Saxon pronouns with <i>h-</i> (<i>hie, him, hera</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomasonKaufman1988284.E2.80.93290_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomasonKaufman1988284.E2.80.93290-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>With the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest of England</a> in 1066, the now norsified Old English language was subject to contact with the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norman" title="Old Norman">Old Norman</a> language, a <a href="/wiki/Romance_language" title="Romance language" class="mw-redirect">Romance language</a> closely related to Modern <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>. The Norman language in England eventually developed into <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_language" title="Anglo-Norman language">Anglo-Norman</a>. Because Norman was spoken primarily by the elites and nobles, while the lower classes continued speaking Anglo-Saxon, the influence of Norman consisted of introducing a wide range of <a href="/wiki/Loanwords" title="Loanwords" class="mw-redirect">loanwords</a> related to politics, legislation and prestigious social domains.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech200639_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech200639-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> Middle English also simplified the inflectional system. The distinction between nominative and accusative case was lost except in personal pronouns, the instrumental case was dropped, and the use of the genitive case was limited to describing <a href="/wiki/Possession_(linguistics)" title="Possession (linguistics)">possession</a>. The inflectional system regularised many irregular inflectional forms,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELass1992_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELass1992-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> and gradually simplified the system of agreement, making word order less flexible.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFischervan_der_Wurff2006111.E2.80.9313_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFischervan_der_Wurff2006111.E2.80.9313-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> By the <a href="/wiki/Wycliffe_Bible" title="Wycliffe Bible" class="mw-redirect">Wycliffe Bible</a> of the 1380s, the passage Matthew 8:20 was written</p>
<dl>
<dd><i>Foxis han dennes, and briddis of heuene han nestis</i><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Here the plural suffix <i>-n</i> on the verb <i>have</i> is still retained, but none of the case endings on the nouns are present.</p>
<p>By the 12th century Middle English was fully developed, integrating both Norse and Norman features; it continued to be spoken until the transition to early Modern English around 1500. Middle English literature includes <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Canterbury_Tales" title="The Canterbury Tales">The Canterbury Tales</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Malory" title="Thomas Malory">Malory</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Morte_d%27Arthur" title="Le Morte d'Arthur">Le Morte d'Arthur</a></i>. In the Middle English period the use of regional dialects in writing proliferated, and dialect traits were even used for effect by authors such as Chaucer.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Modern_English">Early Modern English</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_English" title="Early Modern English">Early Modern English</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Vowel_Shift2a.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Great_Vowel_Shift2a.svg/300px-Great_Vowel_Shift2a.svg.png" width="300" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Great_Vowel_Shift2a.svg/450px-Great_Vowel_Shift2a.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Great_Vowel_Shift2a.svg/600px-Great_Vowel_Shift2a.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="560" data-file-height="315" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Vowel_Shift2a.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Graphic representation of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Vowel_Shift" title="Great Vowel Shift">Great Vowel Shift</a>, showing how the pronunciation of the long vowels gradually shifted, with the high vowels i: and u: breaking into diphthongs and the lower vowels each shifting their pronunciation up one level.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The next period in the history of English was Early Modern English (1500–1700). Early Modern English was characterised by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Vowel_Shift" title="Great Vowel Shift">Great Vowel Shift</a> (1350–1700), inflectional simplification, and linguistic standardisation.</p>
<p>The Great Vowel Shift affected the stressed long vowels of Middle English. It was a <a href="/wiki/Chain_shift" title="Chain shift">chain shift</a>, meaning that each shift triggered a subsequent shift in the vowel system. <a href="/wiki/Mid_vowel" title="Mid vowel">Mid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Open_vowel" title="Open vowel">open vowels</a> were <a href="/wiki/Raising_(phonology)" title="Raising (phonology)" class="mw-redirect">raised</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Close_vowel" title="Close vowel">close vowels</a> were <a href="/wiki/Vowel_breaking" title="Vowel breaking">broken</a> into <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthongs</a>. For example, the word <i>bite</i> was originally pronounced as the word <i>beet</i> is today, and the second vowel in the word <i>about</i> was pronounced as the word <i>boot</i> is today. The Great Vowel Shift explains many irregularities in spelling, since English retains many spellings from Middle English, and it also explains why English vowel letters have very different pronunciations from the same letters in other languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELass2000_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELass2000-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEG.C3.B6rlach199166.E2.80.9370_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEG.C3.B6rlach199166.E2.80.9370-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>English began to rise in prestige during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Henry_V_of_England" title="Henry V of England">Henry V</a>. Around 1430, the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Chancery" title="Court of Chancery">Court of Chancery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Westminster" title="Westminster">Westminster</a> began using English in its <a href="/wiki/Writ" title="Writ">official documents</a>, and a new standard form of Middle English, known as <a href="/wiki/Middle_English#Chancery_Standard" title="Middle English">Chancery Standard</a>, developed from the dialects of London and the <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands_English" title="East Midlands English">East Midlands</a>. In 1476, <a href="/wiki/William_Caxton" title="William Caxton">William Caxton</a> introduced the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> to England and began publishing the first printed books in London, expanding the influence of this form of English.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENevalainenTieken-Boon_van_Ostade2006274.E2.80.9379_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENevalainenTieken-Boon_van_Ostade2006274.E2.80.9379-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> Literature from the Early Modern period includes the works of <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a> and the <a href="/wiki/King_James_Version" title="King James Version">translation of the Bible</a> commissioned by <a href="/wiki/James_VI_and_I" title="James VI and I">King James I</a>. Even after the vowel shift the language still sounded different from Modern English: for example, the <a href="/wiki/Consonant_cluster" title="Consonant cluster">consonant clusters</a> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/kn gn sw/</span> in <i>knight</i>, <i>gnat</i>, and <i>sword</i> were still pronounced. Many of the grammatical features that a modern reader of Shakespeare might find quaint or archaic represent the distinct characteristics of Early Modern English.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECercignani1981_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECercignani1981-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1611 King James Version of the Bible, written in Early Modern English, Matthew 8:20 says:</p>
<dl>
<dd><i>The Foxes haue holes and the birds of the ayre haue nests</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELass200646.E2.80.9347_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELass200646.E2.80.9347-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>This exemplifies the loss of case and its effects on sentence structure (replacement with Subject-Verb-Object word order, and the use of <i>of</i> instead of the non-possessive genitive), and the introduction of loanwords from French (<i>ayre</i>) and word replacements (<i>bird</i> originally meaning "nestling" had replaced OE <i>fugol</i>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread_of_Modern_English">Spread of Modern English</span></h3>
<p>By the late 18th century, the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> had facilitated the spread of English through its colonies and geopolitical dominance. Commerce, science and technology, diplomacy, art, and formal education all contributed to English becoming the first truly global language. English also facilitated worldwide international communication.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHow_English_evolved_into_a_global_language2010_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHow_English_evolved_into_a_global_language2010-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThe_Routes_of_English_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThe_Routes_of_English-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> As England continued to form new colonies, these in turn became independent and developed their own norms for how to speak and write the language. English was adopted in North America, India, parts of Africa, Australasia, and many other regions. In the post-colonial period, some of the newly created nations that had multiple <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_language" title="Indigenous language">indigenous languages</a> opted to continue using English as the official language to avoid the political difficulties inherent in promoting any one indigenous language above the others.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine2006586_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine2006586-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMufwene2006614_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMufwene2006614-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthrup201381.E2.80.9386_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthrup201381.E2.80.9386-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> In the 20th century the growing economic and cultural influence of the United States and its status as a <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">superpower</a> following the Second World War has, along with worldwide broadcasting in English by the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> and other broadcasters, significantly accelerated the spread of the language across the planet.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraddol2006_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraddol2006-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> By the 21st century, English was more widely spoken and written than any language has ever been.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCrumMacNeilCran20039.E2.80.9310_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCrumMacNeilCran20039.E2.80.9310-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A major feature in the early development of Modern English was the codification of explicit norms for standard usage, and their dissemination through official media such as public education and state sponsored publications. In 1755 <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a> published his <i><a href="/wiki/A_Dictionary_of_the_English_Language" title="A Dictionary of the English Language">A Dictionary of the English Language</a></i> which introduced a standard set of spelling conventions and usage norms. In 1828, <a href="/wiki/Noah_Webster" title="Noah Webster">Noah Webster</a> published the <i><a href="/wiki/Webster%27s_Dictionary" title="Webster's Dictionary">American Dictionary of the English language</a></i> in an effort to establish a norm for speaking and writing American English that was independent from the British standard. Within Britain, non-standard or lower class dialect features were increasingly stigmatised, leading to the quick spread of the prestige varieties among the middle classes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine19991.E2.80.9356_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine19991.E2.80.9356-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In terms of grammatical evolution, Modern English has now reached a stage where the loss of case is almost complete (case is now only found in pronouns, such as <i>he</i> and <i>him</i>, <i>she</i> and <i>her</i>, <i>who</i> and <i>whom</i>), and where SVO word-order is mostly fixed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine19991.E2.80.9356_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine19991.E2.80.9356-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> Some changes, such as the use of <a href="/wiki/Do-support" title="Do-support">do-support</a> have become universalised. (Earlier English did not use the word "do" as a general auxiliary as Modern English does; at first it was only used in question constructions where it was not obligatory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine19992_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine19992-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> Now, do-support with the verb <i>have</i> is becoming increasingly standardised.) The use of progressive forms in <i>-ing</i>, appears to be spreading to new constructions, and forms such as <i>had been being built</i> are becoming more common. Regularisation of irregular forms also slowly continues (e.g. <i>dreamed</i> instead of <i>dreamt</i>), and analytical alternatives to inflectional forms are becoming more common (e.g. <i>more polite</i> instead of <i>politer</i>). British English is also undergoing change under the influence of American English, fuelled by the strong presence of American English in the media and the prestige associated with the US as a world power. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeechHundtMairSmith200918.E2.80.9319_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeechHundtMairSmith200918.E2.80.9319-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMairLeech2006_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMairLeech2006-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMair2006_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMair2006-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographical_distribution">Geographical distribution</span></h2>
<p><span id="Geographic_distribution"></span></p>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_territorial_entities_where_English_is_an_official_language" title="List of territorial entities where English is an official language">List of territorial entities where English is an official language</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_English-speaking_population" title="List of countries by English-speaking population">List of countries by English-speaking population</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglosphere" title="Anglosphere">Anglosphere</a></div>
<p>As of 2010 359&#160;million people spoke English as their <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a>. English is probably the third largest language by number of native speakers, after <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Mandarin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEthnologue2010_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEthnologue2010-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> However, when combining native and non-native speakers it is probably the most commonly spoken language in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCrumMacNeilCran20039.E2.80.9310_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCrumMacNeilCran20039.E2.80.9310-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a69_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a69-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> English is spoken by communities on every continent and on oceanic islands in all the major oceans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003b106_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003b106-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> The countries in which English is spoken can be grouped into different categories by how English is used in each country. The "inner circle"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech20062_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech20062-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> countries with many native speakers of English share an international standard of written English and jointly influence speech norms of English around the world. English does not belong to just one country, and it does not belong solely to descendants of English settlers. English is an official language of countries populated by few descendants of native speakers of English. It has also become by far the most important language of international communication when people who share no native language meet anywhere in the world.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Three_circles_of_English-speaking_countries">Three circles of English-speaking countries</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Braj_Kachru" title="Braj Kachru">Braj Kachru</a> distinguishes countries where English is spoken with a <a href="/wiki/World_Englishes#Kachru.27s_Three_Circles_of_English" title="World Englishes">three circles model</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech20062_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech20062-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> In his model, the "inner circle" countries are countries with large communities of native speakers of English, "outer circle" countries have small communities of native speakers of English but widespread use of English as a second language in education or broadcasting or for local official purposes, and "expanding circle" countries are countries where many learners learn English as a foreign language. Kachru bases his model on the history of how English spread in different countries, how users acquire English, and the range of uses English has in each country. The three circles change membership over time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKachru2006196_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKachru2006196-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Countries with large communities of native speakers of English (the inner circle) include Britain, the United States, Australia, Canada, Ireland, and New Zealand, where the majority speaks English, and South Africa, where a significant minority speaks English. The countries with the most native English speakers are, in descending order, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> (at least 231&#160;million),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan2013Table_1_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan2013Table_1-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> (60&#160;million),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffice_for_National_Statistics2013Key_Points_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffice_for_National_Statistics2013Key_Points-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENational_Records_of_Scotland2013_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENational_Records_of_Scotland2013-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthern_Ireland_Statistics_and_Research_Agency2012Table_KS207NI:_Main_Language_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthern_Ireland_Statistics_and_Research_Agency2012Table_KS207NI:_Main_Language-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Canada (19&#160;million),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStatistics_Canada2014_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStatistics_Canada2014-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> (at least 17&#160;million),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAustralian_Bureau_of_Statistics2013_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAustralian_Bureau_of_Statistics2013-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> South Africa (4.8&#160;million),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStatistics_South_Africa2012Table_2.5_Population_by_first_language_spoken_and_province_.28number.29_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStatistics_South_Africa2012Table_2.5_Population_by_first_language_spoken_and_province_.28number.29-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> (4.2&#160;million), and New Zealand (3.7&#160;million).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStatistics_New_Zealand2014_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStatistics_New_Zealand2014-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> In these countries, children of native speakers learn English from their parents, and local people who speak other languages or new immigrants learn English to communicate in their neighbourhoods and workplaces.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBao2006377_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBao2006377-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> The inner-circle countries provide the base from which English spreads to other countries in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKachru2006196_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKachru2006196-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Estimates of the number of English speakers who are <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">second language</a> and foreign-language speakers vary greatly from 470&#160;million to more than 1,000 million depending on how proficiency is defined.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003b108.E2.80.93109_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003b108.E2.80.93109-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Linguist <a href="/wiki/David_Crystal" title="David Crystal">David Crystal</a> estimates that non-native speakers now outnumber native speakers by a ratio of 3 to 1.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a69_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a69-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> In Kachru's three-circles model, the "outer circle" countries are countries such as the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERubino2006_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERubino2006-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatrick2006a_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatrick2006a-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> India, Pakistan, Singapore,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELimAnsaldo2006_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELimAnsaldo2006-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConnell2006_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConnell2006-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchneider2007_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchneider2007-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> with a much smaller proportion of native speakers of English but much use of English as a second language for education, government, or domestic business, and where English is routinely used for school instruction and official interactions with the government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20085_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20085-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> Those countries have millions of native speakers of <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continua</a> ranging from an <a href="/wiki/English-based_creole_languages" title="English-based creole languages">English-based creole</a> to a more standard version of English. They have many more speakers of English who acquire English in the process of growing up through day by day use and listening to broadcasting, especially if they attend schools where English is the medium of instruction. Varieties of English learned by speakers who are not native speakers born to English-speaking parents may be influenced, especially in their grammar, by the other languages spoken by those learners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBao2006377_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBao2006377-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> Most of those varieties of English include words little used by native speakers of English in the inner-circle countries,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBao2006377_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBao2006377-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> and they may have grammatical and phonological differences from inner-circle varieties as well. The standard English of the inner-circle countries is often taken as a norm for use of English in the outer-circle countries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBao2006377_70-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBao2006377-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the three-circles model, countries such as Poland, China, Brazil, Germany, Japan, Indonesia, Egypt, and other countries where English is taught as a foreign language make up the "expanding circle".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20084_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20084-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> The distinctions between English as a first language, as a second language, and as a foreign language are often debatable and may change in particular countries over time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20085_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20085-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> For example, in the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> and some other countries of Europe, knowledge of English as a second language is nearly universal, with over 80 percent of the population able to use it,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEuropean_Commission2012_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEuropean_Commission2012-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> and thus English is routinely used to communicate with foreigners and often in higher education. In these countries, although English is not used for government business, the widespread use of English in these countries puts them at the boundary between the "outer circle" and "expanding circle". English is unusual among world languages in how many of its users are not native speakers but speakers of English as a second or foreign language.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKachru2006197_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKachru2006197-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> Many users of English in the expanding circle use it to communicate with other people from the expanding circle, so that interaction with native speakers of English plays no part in their decision to use English.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKachru2006198_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKachru2006198-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Non-native varieties of English are widely used for international communication, and speakers of one such variety often encounter features of other varieties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBao2006_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBao2006-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> Very often today a conversation in English anywhere in the world may include no native speakers of English at all, even while including speakers from several different countries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20087_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20087-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pluricentric_English">Pluricentric English</span></h3>
<p>English is a <a href="/wiki/Pluricentric_language" title="Pluricentric language">pluricentric language</a>, which means that no one national authority sets the standard for use of the language.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20082_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20082-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine1999_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine1999-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaughCable2002_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaughCable2002-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20088.E2.80.939_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20088.E2.80.939-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> But English is not a divided language,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmmon20081539_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmmon20081539-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> despite a long-standing joke that the United Kingdom and the United States are "two countries divided by a common language". Spoken English, for example English used in broadcasting, generally follows national pronunciation standards that are also established by custom rather than by regulation. International broadcasters are usually identifiable as coming from one country rather than another through their <a href="/wiki/Accent_(sociolinguistics)" title="Accent (sociolinguistics)">accents</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgill2006_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgill2006-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> but newsreader scripts are also composed largely in international <a href="/wiki/Standard_written_English" title="Standard written English">standard written English</a>. The norms of standard written English are maintained purely by the consensus of educated English-speakers around the world, without any oversight by any government or international organisation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmmon20081537.E2.80.931539_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmmon20081537.E2.80.931539-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> American listeners generally readily understand most British broadcasting, and British listeners readily understand most American broadcasting. Most English speakers around the world can understand radio programmes, television programmes, and films from many parts of the English-speaking world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech2006122_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech2006122-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> Both standard and nonstandard varieties of English can include both formal or informal styles, distinguished by word choice and syntax and use both technical and non-technical registers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20085.E2.80.936_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrudgillHannah20085.E2.80.936-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The settlement history of the English-speaking inner circle countries outside Britain helped level dialect distinctions and produce a <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koiné language">koineised</a> form of English in South Africa, Australia, and New Zealand.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeumert2006130_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeumert2006130-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> The majority of immigrants to the United States without British ancestry rapidly adopted English after arrival. Now the majority of the United States population are monolingual English speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeumert2006131_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeumert2006131-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERyan2013Table_1_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERyan2013Table_1-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="English_as_a_global_language">English as a global language</span></h3>
<p>English has ceased to be an "English language" in the sense of belonging only to people who are ethnically English.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine19995_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine19995-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech20061_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech20061-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Use of English is growing country-by-country internally and for international communication. Most people learn English for practical rather than ideological reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKachru2006195_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKachru2006195-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> Many speakers of English in Africa have become part of an "Afro-Saxon" language community that unites Africans from different countries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMazruiMazrui1998_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMazruiMazrui1998-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As decolonisation proceeded throughout the British Empire in the 1950s and 1960s, former colonies often did not reject English but rather continued to use it as independent countries setting their own language policies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMufwene2006614_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMufwene2006614-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMesthrie2010594_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMesthrie2010594-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthrup201381.E2.80.9386_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthrup201381.E2.80.9386-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> For example, the view of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_English" title="Indian English">English language</a> among many Indians has gone from associating it with colonialism to associating it with economic progress, and English continues to be an official language of India.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnnamalai2006_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnnamalai2006-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> English is also widely used in media and literature, and the number of English language books published annually in India is the third largest in the world after the US and UK.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESailaja20092.E2.80.939_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESailaja20092.E2.80.939-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> David Crystal claimed in 2004 that, combining native and non-native speakers, India now has more people who speak or understand English than any other country in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2004b_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2004b-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> but the number of English speakers in India is very uncertain, with most scholars concluding that the United States still has more speakers of English than India.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraddol2010_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraddol2010-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Modern English, sometimes described as the first global <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeierkord2006165_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeierkord2006165-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraddol2006_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraddol2006-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> is also regarded as the first <a href="/wiki/World_language" title="World language">world language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrutt-Griffler2006690.E2.80.9391_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrutt-Griffler2006690.E2.80.9391-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthrup2013_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthrup2013-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> English is the world's most widely used language in newspaper publishing, book publishing, international telecommunications, scientific publishing, international trade, mass entertainment, and diplomacy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthrup2013_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthrup2013-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> English is, by international treaty, the basis for the required <a href="/wiki/Controlled_natural_language" title="Controlled natural language">controlled natural languages</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWojcik2006139_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWojcik2006139-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Seaspeak" title="Seaspeak">Seaspeak</a> and Airspeak used as <a href="/wiki/International_auxiliary_language" title="International auxiliary language">international languages</a> of seafaring<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInternational_Maritime_Organization2011_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInternational_Maritime_Organization2011-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> and aviation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInternational_Civil_Aviation_Organization2011_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInternational_Civil_Aviation_Organization2011-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> English has replaced German as the dominant language of scientific research.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGordin2015_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGordin2015-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> It achieved parity with <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> as a language of diplomacy at the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a> negotiations in 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipson200447_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipson200447-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> By the time of the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> at the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, English had become pre-eminent <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConradRubal-Lopez1996261_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConradRubal-Lopez1996261-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> and is now the main worldwide language of diplomacy and international relations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichter201229_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichter201229-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> It is one of six official languages of the United Nations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnited_Nations2008_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnited_Nations2008-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> Many other worldwide international organisations, including the <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">International Olympic Committee</a>, specify English as a working language or official language of the organisation.</p>
<p>Many regional international organisations such as the <a href="/wiki/European_Free_Trade_Association" title="European Free Trade Association">European Free Trade Association</a>, <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Southeast_Asian_Nations" title="Association of Southeast Asian Nations">Association of Southeast Asian Nations</a> (ASEAN),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Asia-Pacific_Economic_Cooperation" title="Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation">Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation</a> (APEC) set English as their organisation's sole working language even though most members are not countries with a majority of native English speakers. While the European Union (EU) allows member states to designate any of the national languages as an official language of the Union, in practice English is the main working language of EU organisations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAmmon2006321_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAmmon2006321-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Although in most countries English is not an official language, it is currently the language most often taught as a <a href="/wiki/Foreign_language" title="Foreign language">foreign language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraddol2006_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraddol2006-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2003a-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> In the countries of the EU, English is the most widely spoken foreign language in nineteen of the twenty-five member states where it is not an official language (that is, the countries other than the UK and Ireland). In a 2012 official Eurobarometer poll, 38 percent of the EU respondents outside the countries where English is an official language said they could speak English well enough to have a conversation in that language. The next most commonly mentioned foreign language, French (which is the most widely known foreign language in the UK and Ireland), could be used in conversation by 12 percent of respondents.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEuropean_Commission201221.2C_19_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEuropean_Commission201221.2C_19-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A working knowledge of English has become a requirement in a number of occupations and professions such as medicine<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlcaraz_ArizaNavarro2006_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlcaraz_ArizaNavarro2006-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> and computing. English has become so important in scientific publishing that more than 80 percent of all scientific journal articles indexed by <i>Chemical Abstracts</i> in 1998 were written in English, as were 90 percent of all articles in natural science publications by 1996 and 82 percent of articles in humanities publications by 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrutt-Griffler2006694.E2.80.9395_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrutt-Griffler2006694.E2.80.9395-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The increased use of the English language globally has had an effect on other languages, leading to some English words being assimilated into the vocabularies of other languages. This influence of English has led to concerns about <a href="/wiki/Language_death" title="Language death">language death</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2002_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2002-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> and to claims of <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_imperialism" title="Linguistic imperialism">linguistic imperialism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJambor2007_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJambor2007-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> and has provoked resistance to the spread of English; however the number of speakers continues to increase because many people around the world think that English provides them with opportunities for better employment and improved lives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech2006Chapter_12:_English_into_the_Future_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESvartvikLeech2006Chapter_12:_English_into_the_Future-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Although some scholars mention a possibility of future divergence of English dialects into mutually unintelligible languages, most think a more likely outcome is that English will continue to function as a <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koiné language">koineised</a> language in which the standard form unifies speakers from around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrystal2006_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrystal2006-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> English is used as the language for wider communication in countries around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrutt-Griffler2006_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrutt-Griffler2006-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> Thus English has grown in worldwide use much more than any <a href="/wiki/Constructed_language" title="Constructed language">constructed language</a> proposed as an <a href="/wiki/International_auxiliary_language" title="International auxiliary language">international auxiliary language</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Esperanto" title="Esperanto">Esperanto</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELi2003_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELi2003-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeierkord2006163_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeierkord2006163-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_phonology" title="English phonology">English phonology</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">phonetics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a> of English differ between dialects, usually without interfering with mutual communication. Phonological variation affects the inventory of <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemes</a> (speech sounds that distinguish meaning), and phonetic variation is differences in pronunciation of the phonemes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWolfram2006334.E2.80.93335_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWolfram2006334.E2.80.93335-125"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> This overview mainly describes the <a href="/wiki/Standard_language" title="Standard language">standard pronunciations</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>: <a href="/wiki/Received_Pronunciation" title="Received Pronunciation">Received Pronunciation</a> (RP) and <a href="/wiki/General_American" title="General American">General American</a> (GA) (See <a href="/wiki/English_language#Dialects.2C_accents.2C_and_varieties" title="English language">Section below on "Dialects, accents and varieties"</a>). The phonetic symbols used below are from the <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">International Phonetic Alphabet</a> (IPA).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarrHoneybone2007_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarrHoneybone2007-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBerm.C3.BAdez-OteroMcMahon2006_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBerm.C3.BAdez-OteroMcMahon2006-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacMahon2006_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacMahon2006-128"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants">Consonants</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_phonology#Consonants" title="English phonology">English phonology § Consonants</a></div>
<p>Most English dialects share the same 24 consonant phonemes. The consonant inventory shown below is valid for Californian American English,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInternational_Phonetic_Association199941.E2.80.9342_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInternational_Phonetic_Association199941.E2.80.9342-129"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> and for RP.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6nig1994534_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEK.C3.B6nig1994534-130"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<td style="border-left: 0;"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">r</span></td>
